<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Alkane</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Alkene" title="Alkene">Alkene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">Alkali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alkyne" title="Alkyne">Alkyne</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkaline" title="Alkaline">Alkaline</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Methane-2D-stereo.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="528" data-file-width="512" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Methane-2D-stereo.svg/220px-Methane-2D-stereo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Methane-2D-stereo.svg/330px-Methane-2D-stereo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Methane-2D-stereo.svg/440px-Methane-2D-stereo.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Methane-2D-stereo.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chemical structure of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, the simplest alkane</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a>, an <b>alkane</b>, or <b>paraffin</b> (a historical name that also has <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Paraffin_(disambiguation)" title="Paraffin (disambiguation)">other meanings</a>), is an <a href="/wiki/Open-chain_compound" title="Open-chain compound">acyclic</a> <a href="/wiki/Saturation_(chemistry)" title="Saturation (chemistry)">saturated</a> <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a>. In other words, an alkane consists of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> atoms arranged in a <a href="/wiki/Tree_(graph_theory)" title="Tree (graph theory)">tree</a> structure in which all the <a href="/wiki/Carbon%E2%80%93carbon_bond" title="Carbon–carbon bond">carbon–carbon bonds</a> are <a href="/wiki/Single_bond" title="Single bond">single</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goldbook-alkanes_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-goldbook-alkanes-1">[1]</a></sup> Alkanes have the general chemical formula C<sub><i>n</i></sub>H<sub>2<i>n</i>+2</sub>. The alkanes range in complexity from the simplest case of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (CH<sub>4</sub>), where <i>n</i> = 1 (sometimes called the parent molecule), to arbitrarily large and complex molecules, like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pentacontane" title="Pentacontane">pentacontane</a> (C<sub>50</sub>H<sub>102</sub>) or 6-ethyl-2-methyl-5-(1-methylethyl)octane, an <a href="/wiki/Isomer" title="Isomer">isomer</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tetradecane" title="Tetradecane">tetradecane</a> (C<sub>14</sub>H<sub>30</sub>)
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUPAC" title="IUPAC">IUPAC</a> defines alkanes as "acyclic branched or unbranched hydrocarbons having the general formula <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">C</a><sub><i>n</i></sub><a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">H</a><sub>2<i>n</i>+2</sub>, and therefore consisting entirely of hydrogen atoms and saturated carbon atoms". However, some sources use the term to denote <i>any</i> saturated hydrocarbon, including those that are either monocyclic (i.e. the <a href="/wiki/Cycloalkane" title="Cycloalkane">cycloalkanes</a>) or polycyclic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> despite their having a different general formula (i.e. cycloalkanes are C<sub><i>n</i></sub>H<sub>2<i>n</i></sub>).
</p><p>In an alkane, each carbon atom is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_hybridization" title="Orbital hybridization">sp<sup>3</sup>-hybridized</a> with 4 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sigma_bonds" title="Sigma bonds">sigma bonds</a> (either C–C or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C%E2%80%93H_bond" title="C–H bond">C–H</a>), and each hydrogen atom is joined to one of the carbon atoms (in a C–H bond). The longest series of linked carbon atoms in a molecule is known as its <a href="/wiki/Skeletal_formula" title="Skeletal formula">carbon skeleton</a> or carbon backbone. The number of carbon atoms may be thought of as the size of the alkane.
</p><p>One group of the <a href="/wiki/Higher_alkanes" title="Higher alkanes">higher alkanes</a> are <a href="/wiki/Wax" title="Wax">waxes</a>, solids at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_ambient_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard ambient temperature and pressure">standard ambient temperature and pressure</a> (SATP), for which the number of carbons in the carbon backbone is greater than about 17.
With their repeated –CH<sub>2</sub> units, the alkanes constitute a <a href="/wiki/Homologous_series" title="Homologous series">homologous series</a> of organic compounds in which the members differ in <a href="/wiki/Molecular_mass" title="Molecular mass">molecular mass</a> by multiples of 14.03 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_unit" title="Atomic mass unit">u</a> (the total mass of each such <a href="/wiki/Methylene_bridge" title="Methylene bridge">methylene-bridge</a> unit, which comprises a single carbon atom of mass 12.01 u and two hydrogen atoms of mass ~1.01 u each).
</p><p>Alkanes are not very reactive and have little <a href="/wiki/Biological_activity" title="Biological activity">biological activity</a>. They can be viewed as molecular trees upon which can be hung the more active/reactive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Functional_groups" title="Functional groups">functional groups</a> of biological molecules.
</p><p>The alkanes have two main commercial sources: <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> (crude oil)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-google-3">[3]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>.
</p><p>An <b><a href="/wiki/Alkyl" title="Alkyl">alkyl</a> group</b>, generally abbreviated with the symbol R, is a functional group that, like an alkane, consists solely of single-bonded carbon and hydrogen atoms connected acyclically—for example, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methyl" title="Methyl">methyl</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ethyl_group" title="Ethyl group">ethyl group</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_classification">Structure classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Structure classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Saturated hydrocarbons are <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> having only single covalent bonds between their carbons. They can be:
</p>
<ul><li>linear (general formula <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"><i>n</i></sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2<i>n</i>+2</sub></span></span>) wherein the carbon atoms are joined in a snake-like structure</li>
<li>branched (general formula <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"><i>n</i></sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2<i>n</i>+2</sub></span></span>, <i>n</i> &gt; 2) wherein the carbon backbone splits off in one or more directions</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cyclic_compound" title="Cyclic compound">cyclic</a> (general formula <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"><i>n</i></sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2<i>n</i></sub></span></span>, <i>n</i> &gt; 3) wherein the carbon backbone is linked so as to form a loop.</li></ul>
<p>According to the definition by <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">IUPAC</a>, the former two are alkanes, whereas the third group is called <a href="/wiki/Cycloalkane" title="Cycloalkane">cycloalkanes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Saturated hydrocarbons can also combine any of the linear, cyclic (e.g., polycyclic) and branching structures; the general formula is  <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"><i>n</i></sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2<i>n</i>−2<i>k</i>+2</sub></span></span>, where <i>k</i> is the number of independent loops. Alkanes are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_chain_compound" title="Open chain compound">acyclic</a> (loopless) ones, corresponding to <i>k</i> = 0.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Isomerism">Isomerism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Isomerism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturated_C4_hydrocarbons_ball-and-stick.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1362" height="258" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Saturated_C4_hydrocarbons_ball-and-stick.png/220px-Saturated_C4_hydrocarbons_ball-and-stick.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Saturated_C4_hydrocarbons_ball-and-stick.png/330px-Saturated_C4_hydrocarbons_ball-and-stick.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Saturated_C4_hydrocarbons_ball-and-stick.png/440px-Saturated_C4_hydrocarbons_ball-and-stick.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturated_C4_hydrocarbons_ball-and-stick.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Different C<sub>4</sub> alkanes and cycloalkanes (left to right): <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N-butane" title="N-butane"><i>n</i>-butane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isobutane" title="Isobutane">isobutane</a> are the two C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>10</sub> isomers; <a href="/wiki/Cyclobutane" title="Cyclobutane">cyclobutane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methylcyclopropane" title="Methylcyclopropane">methylcyclopropane</a> are the two C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>8</sub> alkane isomers.<br/> <a href="/wiki/Bicyclobutane" title="Bicyclobutane">Bicyclo[1.1.0]butane</a> is the only C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>6</sub> alkane and has no alkane isomer; <a href="/wiki/Tetrahedrane" title="Tetrahedrane">tetrahedrane</a> (below) is the only C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>4</sub> alkane and so has no alkane isomer.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tetrahedrane-3D-balls.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1058" data-file-width="1100" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Tetrahedrane-3D-balls.png/100px-Tetrahedrane-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Tetrahedrane-3D-balls.png/150px-Tetrahedrane-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Tetrahedrane-3D-balls.png/200px-Tetrahedrane-3D-balls.png 2x" width="100"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tetrahedrane-3D-balls.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tetrahedrane" title="Tetrahedrane">Tetrahedrane</a></div></div></div>
<p>Alkanes with more than three <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> atoms can be arranged in various different ways, forming <a href="/wiki/Structural_isomer" title="Structural isomer">structural isomers</a>. The simplest isomer of an alkane is the one in which the carbon atoms are arranged in a single chain with no branches. This isomer is sometimes called the <i>n</i>-isomer (<i>n</i> for "normal", although it is not necessarily the most common). However the chain of carbon atoms may also be branched at one or more points. The number of possible isomers increases rapidly with the number of carbon atoms. For example, for acyclic alkanes:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OEIS_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-OEIS-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>C<sub>1</sub>: <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> only</li>
<li>C<sub>2</sub>: <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a> only</li>
<li>C<sub>3</sub>: <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a> only</li>
<li>C<sub>4</sub>: 2 isomers: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N-butane" title="N-butane"><i>n</i>-butane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isobutane" title="Isobutane">isobutane</a></li>
<li>C<sub>5</sub>: 3 isomers: <a href="/wiki/Pentane" title="Pentane">pentane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isopentane" title="Isopentane">isopentane</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neopentane" title="Neopentane">neopentane</a></li>
<li>C<sub>6</sub>: 5 isomers: <a href="/wiki/Hexane" title="Hexane">hexane</a>, <a href="/wiki/2-Methylpentane" title="2-Methylpentane">2-methylpentane</a>, <a href="/wiki/3-Methylpentane" title="3-Methylpentane">3-methylpentane</a>, <a href="/wiki/2,2-Dimethylbutane" title="2,2-Dimethylbutane">2,2-dimethylbutane</a>, and <a href="/wiki/2,3-Dimethylbutane" title="2,3-Dimethylbutane">2,3-dimethylbutane</a></li>
<li>C<sub>12</sub>: 355 isomers</li>
<li>C<sub>32</sub>: 27,711,253,769 isomers</li>
<li>C<sub>60</sub>: 22,158,734,535,770,411,074,184 isomers, many of which are not stable.</li></ul>
<p>Branched alkanes can be <a href="/wiki/Chirality_(chemistry)" title="Chirality (chemistry)">chiral</a>. For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3-methylhexane" title="3-methylhexane">3-methylhexane</a> and its higher <a href="/wiki/Homology_(chemistry)" title="Homology (chemistry)">homologues</a> are chiral due to their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stereogenic_center" title="Stereogenic center">stereogenic center</a> at carbon atom number 3. In addition to the alkane isomers, the chain of carbon atoms may form one or more loops. Such compounds are called <a href="/wiki/Cycloalkane" title="Cycloalkane">cycloalkanes</a>.  Stereoisomers and cyclic compounds are excluded when calculating the number of isomers above.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/IUPAC_nomenclature_of_organic_chemistry" title="IUPAC nomenclature of organic chemistry">IUPAC nomenclature of organic chemistry</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/IUPAC_nomenclature_of_organic_chemistry#Alkanes" title="IUPAC nomenclature of organic chemistry">IUPAC nomenclature</a> (systematic way of naming compounds) for alkanes is based on identifying hydrocarbon chains. Unbranched, saturated hydrocarbon chains are named systematically with a Greek numerical prefix denoting the number of carbons and the suffix "-ane".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1866, <a href="/wiki/August_Wilhelm_von_Hofmann" title="August Wilhelm von Hofmann">August Wilhelm von Hofmann</a> suggested systematizing nomenclature by using the whole sequence of vowels a, e, i, o and u to create suffixes -ane, -ene, -ine (or -yne), -one, -une, for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrocarbons" title="Hydrocarbons">hydrocarbons</a>  C<sub><i>n</i></sub>H<sub>2<i>n</i>+2</sub>, C<sub><i>n</i></sub>H<sub>2<i>n</i></sub>, C<sub><i>n</i></sub>H<sub>2<i>n</i>−2</sub>, C<sub><i>n</i></sub>H<sub>2<i>n</i>−4</sub>, C<sub><i>n</i></sub>H<sub>2<i>n</i>−6</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Now, the first three name hydrocarbons with single, double and triple bonds;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> "-one" represents a <a href="/wiki/Ketone" title="Ketone">ketone</a>; "-ol" represents an alcohol or OH group; "-oxy-" means an <a href="/wiki/Ether" title="Ether">ether</a> and refers to oxygen between two carbons, so that methoxymethane is the IUPAC name for <a href="/wiki/Dimethyl_ether" title="Dimethyl ether">dimethyl ether</a>.
</p><p>It is difficult or impossible to find compounds with more than one <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">IUPAC</a> name. This is because shorter chains attached to longer chains are prefixes and the convention includes brackets. Numbers in the name, referring to which carbon a group is attached to, should be as low as possible so that 1- is implied and usually omitted from names of organic compounds with only one side-group. Symmetric compounds will have two ways of arriving at the same name.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Linear_alkanes">Linear alkanes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Linear alkanes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Straight-chain alkanes are sometimes indicated by the prefix "<i>n</i>-" (for <i>normal</i>) where a non-linear <a href="/wiki/Isomer" title="Isomer">isomer</a> exists. Although this is not strictly necessary, the usage is still common in cases where there is an important difference in properties between the straight-chain and branched-chain isomers, e.g., <a href="/wiki/Hexane" title="Hexane"><i>n</i>-hexane</a> or 2- or 3-methylpentane. Alternative names for this group are: <b>linear paraffins</b> or <b><i>n</i>-paraffins</b>.
</p><p>The members of the series (in terms of number of carbon atoms) are named as follows:
</p>
<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a></dt>
<dd>CH<sub>4</sub> – one carbon and four hydrogen</dd>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a> </dt>
<dd>C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>6</sub> – two carbon and six hydrogen</dd>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a></dt>
<dd>C<sub>3</sub>H<sub>8</sub> – three carbon and 8 hydrogen</dd>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Butane" title="Butane">butane</a> </dt>
<dd>C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>10</sub> – four carbon and 10 hydrogen</dd>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Pentane" title="Pentane">pentane</a></dt>
<dd>C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>12</sub> – five carbon and 12 hydrogen</dd>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Hexane" title="Hexane">hexane</a> </dt>
<dd>C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>14</sub> – six carbon and 14 hydrogen</dd></dl>
<p>The first four names were <a href="/wiki/Back-formation" title="Back-formation">derived</a> from <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_ether" title="Diethyl ether">ether</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propionic_acid" title="Propionic acid">propionic acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Butyric_acid" title="Butyric acid">butyric acid</a>, respectively (<a href="/wiki/Hexadecane" title="Hexadecane">hexadecane</a> is also sometimes referred to as cetane). Alkanes with five or more carbon atoms are named by adding the <a href="/wiki/Affix" title="Affix">suffix</a> <b>-ane</b> to the appropriate <a href="/wiki/IUPAC_numerical_multiplier" title="IUPAC numerical multiplier">numerical multiplier</a> prefix<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reusch-nom_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-reusch-nom-9">[9]</a></sup> with elision of any terminal vowel (<i>-a</i> or <i>-o</i>) from the basic numerical term. Hence, <a href="/wiki/Pentane" title="Pentane">pentane</a>, C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>12</sub>; <a href="/wiki/Hexane" title="Hexane">hexane</a>, C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>14</sub>; <a href="/wiki/Heptane" title="Heptane">heptane</a>, C<sub>7</sub>H<sub>16</sub>; <a href="/wiki/Octane" title="Octane">octane</a>, C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>18</sub>; etc. The prefix is generally Greek, however alkanes with a carbon atom count ending in nine, for example <a href="/wiki/Nonane" title="Nonane">nonane</a>, use the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> prefix <b>non-</b>. For a more complete list, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_alkanes" title="List of alkanes">List of alkanes</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Branched_alkanes">Branched alkanes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Branched alkanes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Isopentane-numbered-3D-balls.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="849" data-file-width="1100" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Isopentane-numbered-3D-balls.png/220px-Isopentane-numbered-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Isopentane-numbered-3D-balls.png/330px-Isopentane-numbered-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Isopentane-numbered-3D-balls.png/440px-Isopentane-numbered-3D-balls.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Isopentane-numbered-3D-balls.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ball-and-stick_model" title="Ball-and-stick model">Ball-and-stick model</a> of <a href="/wiki/Isopentane" title="Isopentane">isopentane</a> (common name) or 2-methylbutane (IUPAC systematic name)</div></div></div>
<p>Simple branched alkanes often have a common name using a prefix to distinguish them from linear alkanes, for example <a href="/wiki/Pentane" title="Pentane"><i>n</i>-pentane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isopentane" title="Isopentane">isopentane</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neopentane" title="Neopentane">neopentane</a>.
</p><p>IUPAC naming conventions can be used to produce a systematic name.
</p><p>The key steps in the naming of more complicated branched alkanes are as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Identify the longest continuous chain of carbon atoms</li>
<li>Name this longest root chain using standard naming rules</li>
<li>Name each side chain by changing the suffix of the name of the alkane from "-ane" to "-yl"</li>
<li>Number the longest continuous chain in order to give the lowest possible numbers for the side-chains <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></li>
<li>Number and name the side chains before the name of the root chain</li>
<li>If there are multiple side chains of the same type, use prefixes such as "di-" and "tri-" to indicate it as such, and number each one.</li>
<li>Add side chain names in alphabetical (disregarding "di-" etc. prefixes) order in front of the name of the root chain</li></ul>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">
<caption>Comparison of nomenclatures for three isomers of C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>12</sub>
</caption>

<th>isopentane</th>
<th>neopentane
</th></tr>
<tr>

</th>
<th>pentane</th>

<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Isopentane-2D-skeletal.png"><img alt="Isopentane-2D-skeletal.png" data-file-height="682" data-file-width="1100" height="56" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Isopentane-2D-skeletal.png/90px-Isopentane-2D-skeletal.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Isopentane-2D-skeletal.png/135px-Isopentane-2D-skeletal.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Isopentane-2D-skeletal.png/180px-Isopentane-2D-skeletal.png 2x" width="90"/></a></td>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Saturated_cyclic_hydrocarbons">Saturated cyclic hydrocarbons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Saturated cyclic hydrocarbons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cycloalkane" title="Cycloalkane">Cycloalkane</a></div>
<p>Though technically distinct from the alkanes, this class of hydrocarbons is referred to by some as the "cyclic alkanes." As their description implies, they contain one or more rings.
</p><p>Simple cycloalkanes have a prefix "cyclo-" to distinguish them from alkanes. Cycloalkanes are named as per their acyclic counterparts with respect to the number of carbon atoms in their backbones, e.g., <a href="/wiki/Cyclopentane" title="Cyclopentane">cyclopentane</a> (C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>10</sub>) is a cycloalkane with 5 carbon atoms just like <a href="/wiki/Pentane" title="Pentane">pentane</a> (C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>12</sub>), but they are joined up in a five-membered ring. In a similar manner, <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyclopropane" title="Cyclopropane">cyclopropane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Butane" title="Butane">butane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyclobutane" title="Cyclobutane">cyclobutane</a>, etc.
</p><p>Substituted cycloalkanes are named similarly to substituted alkanes — the cycloalkane ring is stated, and the substituents are according to their position on the ring, with the numbering decided by the <a href="/wiki/Cahn%E2%80%93Ingold%E2%80%93Prelog_priority_rules" title="Cahn–Ingold–Prelog priority rules">Cahn–Ingold–Prelog priority rules</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reusch-nom_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-reusch-nom-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Trivial.2Fcommon_names"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Trivial/common_names">Trivial/common names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Trivial/common names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The trivial (non-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUPAC_nomenclature" title="IUPAC nomenclature">systematic</a>) name for alkanes is <i>paraffins</i>. Together, alkanes are known as the <i>paraffin series</i>. Trivial names for compounds are usually historical artifacts. They were coined before the development of systematic names, and have been retained due to familiar usage in industry. Cycloalkanes are also called naphthenes.
</p><p>It is almost certain that the term <i>paraffin</i> stems from the <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical_industry" title="Petrochemical industry">petrochemical industry</a>. Branched-chain alkanes are called <i>isoparaffins</i>. The use of the term "paraffin" is a general term and often does not distinguish between pure compounds and mixtures of <a href="/wiki/Isomer" title="Isomer">isomers</a>, i.e., compounds of the same <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">chemical formula</a>, e.g., <a href="/wiki/Pentane" title="Pentane">pentane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isopentane" title="Isopentane">isopentane</a>.
</p>
<dl><dt>Examples</dt></dl>
<p>The following trivial names are retained in the IUPAC system:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Isobutane" title="Isobutane">isobutane</a> for 2-methylpropane</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Isopentane" title="Isopentane">isopentane</a> for 2-methylbutane</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Neopentane" title="Neopentane">neopentane</a> for 2,2-dimethylpropane.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>All alkanes are colorless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s-anand_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-s-anand-13">[13]</a></sup>  Alkanes with the lowest molecular weights are gasses, those of intermediate molecular weight are liquids, and the heaviest are waxy solids.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Table_of_alkanes">Table of alkanes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Table of alkanes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
