<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Boiling_point">Boiling point</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Boiling point">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AlkaneBoilingMeltingPoint.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="374" data-file-width="713" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/AlkaneBoilingMeltingPoint.png/400px-AlkaneBoilingMeltingPoint.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/AlkaneBoilingMeltingPoint.png/600px-AlkaneBoilingMeltingPoint.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/82/AlkaneBoilingMeltingPoint.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AlkaneBoilingMeltingPoint.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Melting (blue) and boiling (orange) points of the first 16 <i>n</i>-alkanes in °C.</div></div></div>
<p>Alkanes experience intermolecular <a href="/wiki/Van_der_Waals_force" title="Van der Waals force">van der Waals forces</a>. Stronger intermolecular van der Waals forces give rise to greater boiling points of alkanes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m&amp;b_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-m&amp;b-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are two determinants for the strength of the van der Waals forces:
</p>
<ul><li>the number of electrons surrounding the <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecule</a>, which increases with the alkane's molecular weight</li>
<li>the surface area of the molecule</li></ul>
<p>Under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_conditions" title="Standard conditions">standard conditions</a>, from CH<sub>4</sub> to C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>10</sub> alkanes are gaseous; from C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>12</sub> to C<sub>17</sub>H<sub>36</sub> they are liquids; and after C<sub>18</sub>H<sub>38</sub> they are solids. As the boiling point of alkanes is primarily determined by weight, it should not be a surprise that the boiling point has almost a linear relationship with the size (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_weight" title="Molecular weight">molecular weight</a>) of the molecule. As a rule of thumb, the boiling point rises 20–30 °C for each carbon added to the chain; this rule applies to other homologous series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m&amp;b_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-m&amp;b-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>A straight-chain alkane will have a boiling point higher than a branched-chain alkane due to the greater surface area in contact, thus the greater van der Waals forces, between adjacent molecules. For example, compare <a href="/wiki/Isobutane" title="Isobutane">isobutane</a> (2-methylpropane) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N-butane" title="N-butane">n-butane</a> (butane), which boil at −12 and 0 °C, and 2,2-dimethylbutane and 2,3-dimethylbutane which boil at 50 and 58 °C, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m&amp;b_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-m&amp;b-14">[14]</a></sup> For the latter case, two molecules 2,3-dimethylbutane can "lock" into each other better than the cross-shaped 2,2-dimethylbutane, hence the greater van der Waals forces.
</p><p>On the other hand, cycloalkanes tend to have higher boiling points than their linear counterparts due to the locked conformations of the molecules, which give a plane of intermolecular contact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Melting_points">Melting points</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Melting points">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting points</a> of the alkanes follow a similar trend to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boiling_points" title="Boiling points">boiling points</a> for the same reason as outlined above. That is, (all other things being equal) the larger the molecule the higher the melting point. There is one significant difference between boiling points and melting points. Solids have more rigid and fixed structure than liquids. This rigid structure requires energy to break down. Thus the better put together solid structures will require more energy to break apart. For alkanes, this can be seen from the graph above (i.e., the blue line). The odd-numbered alkanes have a lower trend in melting points than even numbered alkanes. This is because even numbered alkanes pack well in the solid phase, forming a well-organized structure, which requires more energy to break apart. The odd-numbered alkanes pack less well and so the "looser" organized solid packing structure requires less energy to break apart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The melting points of branched-chain alkanes can be either higher or lower than those of the corresponding straight-chain alkanes, again depending on the ability of the alkane in question to pack well in the solid phase: This is particularly true for <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Isoalkanes&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Isoalkanes (page does not exist)">isoalkanes</a> (2-methyl isomers), which often have melting points higher than those of the linear analogues.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conductivity_and_solubility">Conductivity and solubility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Conductivity and solubility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Alkanes do not conduct electricity in any way, nor are they substantially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relative_static_permittivity" title="Relative static permittivity">polarized</a> by an <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric field</a>. For this reason, they do not form <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonds</a> and are insoluble in polar solvents such as water. Since the hydrogen bonds between individual water molecules are aligned away from an alkane molecule, the coexistence of an alkane and water leads to an increase in molecular order (a reduction in <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a>). As there is no significant bonding between water molecules and alkane molecules, the <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">second law of thermodynamics</a> suggests that this reduction in entropy should be minimized by minimizing the contact between alkane and water: Alkanes are said to be <a href="/wiki/Hydrophobe" title="Hydrophobe">hydrophobic</a> in that they repel water.
</p><p>Their solubility in nonpolar solvents is relatively good, a property that is called <a href="/wiki/Lipophilicity" title="Lipophilicity">lipophilicity</a>. Different alkanes are, for example, miscible in all proportions among themselves.
</p><p>The density of the alkanes usually increases with the number of carbon atoms but remains less than that of water. Hence, alkanes form the upper layer in an alkane–water mixture.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Molecular_geometry">Molecular geometry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Molecular geometry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ch4_hybridization.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="164" data-file-width="178" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Ch4_hybridization.svg/220px-Ch4_hybridization.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Ch4_hybridization.svg/330px-Ch4_hybridization.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Ch4_hybridization.svg/440px-Ch4_hybridization.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ch4_hybridization.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>sp<sup>3</sup>-hybridization in <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The molecular structure of the alkanes directly affects their physical and chemical characteristics. It is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">electron configuration</a> of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, which has four <a href="/wiki/Valence_electron" title="Valence electron">valence electrons</a>. The carbon atoms in alkanes are always sp<sup>3</sup>-hybridized, that is to say that the valence electrons are said to be in four equivalent orbitals derived from the combination of the 2s orbital and the three 2p orbitals. These orbitals, which have identical energies, are arranged spatially in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Tetrahedron" title="Tetrahedron">tetrahedron</a>, the angle of cos<sup>−1</sup>(−<span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">3</span></span>) ≈ 109.47° between them.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bond_lengths_and_bond_angles">Bond lengths and bond angles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Bond lengths and bond angles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An alkane molecule has only C–H and C–C single bonds. The former result from the overlap of an sp<sup>3</sup> orbital of carbon with the 1s orbital of a hydrogen; the latter by the overlap of two sp<sup>3</sup> orbitals on different carbon atoms. The <a href="/wiki/Bond_length" title="Bond length">bond lengths</a> amount to 1.09 × 10<sup>−10</sup> m for a C–H bond and 1.54 × 10<sup>−10</sup> m for a C–C bond.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:174px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ch4-structure.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="178" data-file-width="172" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/55/Ch4-structure.png" width="172"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ch4-structure.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The tetrahedral structure of methane.</div></div></div>
<p>The spatial arrangement of the bonds is similar to that of the four sp<sup>3</sup> orbitals—they are tetrahedrally arranged, with an angle of 109.47° between them. Structural formulae that represent the bonds as being at right angles to one another, while both common and useful, do not correspond with the reality.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conformation">Conformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Conformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alkane_stereochemistry" title="Alkane stereochemistry">Alkane stereochemistry</a></div>
<p>The structural formula and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bond_angle" title="Bond angle">bond angles</a> are not usually sufficient to completely describe the geometry of a molecule. There is a further <a href="/wiki/Degrees_of_freedom_(physics_and_chemistry)" title="Degrees of freedom (physics and chemistry)">degree of freedom</a> for each carbon–carbon bond: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Torsion_angle" title="Torsion angle">torsion angle</a> between the atoms or groups bound to the atoms at each end of the bond. The spatial arrangement described by the torsion angles of the molecule is known as its <a href="/wiki/Conformational_isomerism" title="Conformational isomerism">conformation</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Newman_projection_ethane.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="964" data-file-width="2022" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Newman_projection_ethane.png/200px-Newman_projection_ethane.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Newman_projection_ethane.png/300px-Newman_projection_ethane.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Newman_projection_ethane.png/400px-Newman_projection_ethane.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Newman_projection_ethane.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Newman projections of the two conformations of ethane: eclipsed on the left, staggered on the right.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ethane-rotamers-3D-balls.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="555" data-file-width="1100" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Ethane-rotamers-3D-balls.png/200px-Ethane-rotamers-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Ethane-rotamers-3D-balls.png/300px-Ethane-rotamers-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Ethane-rotamers-3D-balls.png/400px-Ethane-rotamers-3D-balls.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ethane-rotamers-3D-balls.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ball-and-stick_model" title="Ball-and-stick model">Ball-and-stick models</a> of the two rotamers of ethane</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">Ethane</a> forms the simplest case for studying the conformation of alkanes, as there is only one C–C bond. If one looks down the axis of the C–C bond, one will see the so-called <a href="/wiki/Newman_projection" title="Newman projection">Newman projection</a>. The hydrogen atoms on both the front and rear carbon atoms have an angle of 120° between them, resulting from the projection of the base of the tetrahedron onto a flat plane. However, the torsion angle between a given hydrogen atom attached to the front carbon and a given hydrogen atom attached to the rear carbon can vary freely between 0° and 360°. This is a consequence of the free rotation about a carbon–carbon single bond. Despite this apparent freedom, only two limiting conformations are important: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eclipsed" title="Eclipsed">eclipsed</a> conformation and <a href="/wiki/Staggered_conformation" title="Staggered conformation">staggered conformation</a>.
</p><p>The two conformations, also known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rotamer" title="Rotamer">rotamers</a>, differ in energy: The staggered conformation is 12.6 kJ/mol lower in energy (more stable) than the eclipsed conformation (the least stable).
</p><p>This difference in energy between the two conformations, known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Torsion_energy" title="Torsion energy">torsion energy</a>, is low compared to the thermal energy of an ethane molecule at ambient temperature. There is constant rotation about the C–C bond. The time taken for an ethane molecule to pass from one staggered conformation to the next, equivalent to the rotation of one CH<sub>3</sub> group by 120° relative to the other, is of the order of 10<sup>−11</sup> seconds.
</p><p>The case of <a href="/wiki/Higher_alkanes" title="Higher alkanes">higher alkanes</a> is more complex but based on similar principles, with the antiperiplanar conformation always being the most favored around each carbon–carbon bond. For this reason, alkanes are usually shown in a zigzag arrangement in diagrams or in models. The actual structure will always differ somewhat from these idealized forms, as the differences in energy between the conformations are small compared to the thermal energy of the molecules: Alkane molecules have no fixed structural form, whatever the models may suggest.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spectroscopic_properties">Spectroscopic properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Spectroscopic properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Virtually all organic compounds contain carbon–carbon, and carbon–hydrogen bonds, and so show some of the features of alkanes in their spectra. Alkanes are notable for having no other groups, and therefore for the <i>absence</i> of other characteristic spectroscopic features of a different functional group like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcohol_(chemistry)" title="Alcohol (chemistry)">–OH</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">–CHO</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carboxylic_acid" title="Carboxylic acid">–COOH</a> etc.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrared_spectroscopy">Infrared spectroscopy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Infrared spectroscopy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The carbon–hydrogen stretching mode gives a strong absorption between 2850 and 2960 <a href="/wiki/Wavenumber" title="Wavenumber">cm<sup>−1</sup></a>, while the carbon–carbon stretching mode absorbs between 800 and 1300 cm<sup>−1</sup>. The carbon–hydrogen bending modes depend on the nature of the group: methyl groups show bands at 1450 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 1375 cm<sup>−1</sup>, while methylene groups show bands at 1465 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 1450 cm<sup>−1</sup>. Carbon chains with more than four carbon atoms show a weak absorption at around 725 cm<sup>−1</sup>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="NMR_spectroscopy">NMR spectroscopy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: NMR spectroscopy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The proton resonances of alkanes are usually found at <a href="/wiki/Chemical_shift" title="Chemical shift"><i>δ</i><sub>H</sub></a> = 0.5–1.5. The carbon-13 resonances depend on the number of hydrogen atoms attached to the carbon: <i>δ</i><sub>C</sub> = 8–30 (primary, methyl, –CH<sub>3</sub>), 15–55 (secondary, methylene, –CH<sub>2</sub>–), 20–60 (tertiary, methyne, C–H) and quaternary. The carbon-13 resonance of quaternary carbon atoms is characteristically weak, due to the lack of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Overhauser_effect" title="Nuclear Overhauser effect">nuclear Overhauser effect</a> and the long <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relaxation_time" title="Relaxation time">relaxation time</a>, and can be missed in weak samples, or samples that have not been run for a sufficiently long time.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mass_spectrometry">Mass spectrometry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Mass spectrometry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Alkanes have a high <a href="/wiki/Ionization_energy" title="Ionization energy">ionization energy</a>, and the molecular ion is usually weak. The fragmentation pattern can be difficult to interpret, but, in the case of branched chain alkanes, the carbon chain is preferentially cleaved at tertiary or quaternary carbons due to the relative stability of the resulting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_radical" title="Free radical">free radicals</a>. The fragment resulting from the loss of a single methyl group (<i>M</i> − 15) is often absent, and other fragments are often spaced by intervals of fourteen mass units, corresponding to sequential loss of CH<sub>2</sub> groups.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_properties">Chemical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Chemical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Alkanes are only weakly reactive with ionic and other polar substances. The <a href="/wiki/Acid_dissociation_constant" title="Acid dissociation constant">acid dissociation constant</a> (pK<sub>a</sub>) values of all alkanes are above 60, hence they are practically inert to acids and bases (see: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_acid" title="Carbon acid">carbon acids</a>). This inertness is the source of the term <i>paraffins</i> (with the meaning here of "lacking affinity"). In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crude_oil" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a> the alkane molecules have remained chemically unchanged for millions of years.
</p><p>However redox reactions of alkanes, in particular with oxygen and the halogens, are possible as the carbon atoms are in a strongly reduced condition; in the case of methane, the lowest possible oxidation state for carbon (−4) is reached. Reaction with oxygen (<i>if</i> present in sufficient quantity to satisfy the reaction <a href="/wiki/Stoichiometry" title="Stoichiometry">stoichiometry</a>) leads to combustion without any smoke, producing <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and water. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_radical_halogenation" title="Free radical halogenation">Free radical halogenation</a> reactions occur with halogens, leading to the production of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haloalkanes" title="Haloalkanes">haloalkanes</a>. In addition, alkanes have been shown to interact with, and bind to, certain transition metal complexes in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon-hydrogen_bond_activation" title="Carbon-hydrogen bond activation">C–H bond activation</a>.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_radical" title="Free radical">Free radicals</a>, molecules with unpaired electrons, play a large role in most reactions of alkanes, such as cracking and reformation where long-chain alkanes are converted into shorter-chain alkanes and straight-chain alkanes into branched-chain isomers.
</p><p>In highly branched alkanes, the bond angle may differ significantly from the optimal value (109.5°) in order to allow the different groups sufficient space. This causes a tension in the molecule, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steric_hindrance" title="Steric hindrance">steric hindrance</a>, and can substantially increase the reactivity.
</p>
<h3><span id="Reactions_with_oxygen_.28combustion_reaction.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactions_with_oxygen_(combustion_reaction)">Reactions with oxygen (combustion reaction)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Reactions with oxygen (combustion reaction)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>All alkanes react with <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> reaction, although they become increasingly difficult to ignite as the number of carbon atoms increases. The general equation for complete combustion is:
</p>
<dl><dd>C<sub><i>n</i></sub>H<sub>2<i>n</i>+2</sub> + (<span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">3</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">2</span></span><i>n</i> + <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">2</span></span>) O<sub>2</sub> → (<i>n</i> + 1) H<sub>2</sub>O + <i>n</i> CO<sub>2</sub></dd>
<dd>or C<sub><i>n</i></sub>H<sub>2<i>n</i>+2</sub> + (<span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">3<i>n</i> + 1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">2</span></span>) O<sub>2</sub> → (<i>n</i> + 1) H<sub>2</sub>O + <i>n</i> CO<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>In the absence of sufficient oxygen, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> or even <a href="/wiki/Soot" title="Soot">soot</a> can be formed, as shown below:
</p>
<dl><dd>C<sub><i>n</i></sub>H<sub>2<i>n</i>+2</sub> + (<i>n</i> + <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">2</span></span>) <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">O<sub>2</sub></a> → (<i>n</i> + 1) H<sub>2</sub>O + <i>n</i> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">CO</a></dd></dl>
<dl><dd>C<sub><i>n</i></sub>H<sub>2<i>n</i>+2</sub> + (<span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">2</span></span><i>n</i> + <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">2</span></span>) <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">O<sub>2</sub></a> → (<i>n</i> + 1) H<sub>2</sub>O + <i>n</i> <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">C</a></dd></dl>
<p>For example, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 CH<sub>4</sub> + 3 O<sub>2</sub> → 2 CO + 4 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd>
<dd>CH<sub>4</sub> + <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">3</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">2</span></span> O<sub>2</sub> → CO + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>See the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_enthalpy_change_of_formation_(data_table)#Alkanes" title="Standard enthalpy change of formation (data table)">alkane heat of formation table</a> for detailed data.
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_enthalpy_change_of_combustion" title="Standard enthalpy change of combustion">standard enthalpy change of combustion</a>, Δ<sub>c</sub><i>H</i><sup>⊖</sup>, for alkanes increases by about 650 kJ/mol per CH<sub>2</sub> group. Branched-chain alkanes have lower values of Δ<sub>c</sub><i>H</i><sup>⊖</sup> than straight-chain alkanes of the same number of carbon atoms, and so can be seen to be somewhat more stable.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactions_with_halogens">Reactions with halogens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Reactions with halogens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_radical_halogenation" title="Free radical halogenation">Free radical halogenation</a></div>
<p>Alkanes react with <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a> in a so-called <i>free radical halogenation</i> reaction. The hydrogen atoms of the alkane are progressively replaced by halogen atoms. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_radical" title="Free radical">Free radicals</a> are the reactive species that participate in the reaction, which usually leads to a mixture of products. The reaction is highly <a href="/wiki/Exothermic_reaction" title="Exothermic reaction">exothermic</a>, and can lead to an explosion.
</p><p>These reactions are an important industrial route to halogenated hydrocarbons. There are three steps:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Initiation</b> the halogen radicals form by <a href="/wiki/Homolysis_(chemistry)" title="Homolysis (chemistry)">homolysis</a>. Usually, energy in the form of heat or light is required.</li>
<li><b>Chain reaction</b> or <b>Propagation</b> then takes place—the halogen radical abstracts a hydrogen from the alkane to give an alkyl radical. This reacts further.</li>
<li><b>Chain termination</b> where the radicals recombine.</li></ul>
<p>Experiments have shown that all halogenation produces a mixture of all possible isomers, indicating that all hydrogen atoms are susceptible to reaction. The mixture produced, however, is not a statistical mixture: Secondary and tertiary hydrogen atoms are preferentially replaced due to the greater stability of secondary and tertiary free-radicals. An example can be seen in the monobromination of propane:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m&amp;b_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-m&amp;b-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Monobromination_of_propane.png" title="Monobromination of propane"><img alt="Monobromination of propane" data-file-height="361" data-file-width="1604" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Monobromination_of_propane.png/500px-Monobromination_of_propane.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Monobromination_of_propane.png/750px-Monobromination_of_propane.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Monobromination_of_propane.png/1000px-Monobromination_of_propane.png 2x" width="500"/></a></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cracking">Cracking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Cracking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cracking_(chemistry)" title="Cracking (chemistry)">Cracking (chemistry)</a></div>
<p>Cracking breaks larger molecules into smaller ones. This can be done with a thermal or catalytic method. The thermal cracking process follows a <a href="/wiki/Homolysis_(chemistry)" title="Homolysis (chemistry)">homolytic</a> mechanism with formation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free-radical" title="Free-radical">free-radicals</a>. The catalytic cracking process involves the presence of <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acid</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalysts</a> (usually solid acids such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica-alumina" title="Silica-alumina">silica-alumina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zeolite" title="Zeolite">zeolites</a>), which promote a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterolytic_cleavage" title="Heterolytic cleavage">heterolytic</a> (asymmetric) breakage of bonds yielding pairs of ions of opposite charges, usually a <a href="/wiki/Carbocation" title="Carbocation">carbocation</a> and the very unstable <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">hydride</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anion" title="Anion">anion</a>. Carbon-localized free radicals and cations are both highly unstable and undergo processes of chain rearrangement, C–C scission in position <a href="/wiki/Beta_scission" title="Beta scission">beta</a> (i.e., cracking) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intramolecular" title="Intramolecular">intra-</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intermolecular" title="Intermolecular">intermolecular</a> hydrogen transfer or <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">hydride</a> transfer. In both types of processes, the corresponding <a href="/wiki/Reactive_intermediate" title="Reactive intermediate">reactive intermediates</a> (radicals, ions) are permanently regenerated, and thus they proceed by a self-propagating chain mechanism. The chain of reactions is eventually terminated by radical or ion recombination.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isomerization_and_reformation">Isomerization and reformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Isomerization and reformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Dragan and his colleague were the first to report about isomerization in alkanes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asinger,_Friedrich_1967_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asinger,_Friedrich_1967-17">[17]</a></sup>  Isomerization and reformation are processes in which straight-chain alkanes are heated in the presence of a <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> catalyst. In isomerization, the alkanes become branched-chain isomers. In other words, it does not lose any carbons or hydrogens, keeping the same molecular weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asinger,_Friedrich_1967_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Asinger,_Friedrich_1967-17">[17]</a></sup> In reformation, the alkanes become <a href="/wiki/Cycloalkane" title="Cycloalkane">cycloalkanes</a> or <a href="/wiki/Aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Aromatic hydrocarbon">aromatic hydrocarbons</a>, giving off hydrogen as a by-product. Both of these processes raise the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Octane_number" title="Octane number">octane number</a> of the substance. Butane is the most common alkane that is put under the process of isomerization, as it makes many branched alkanes with high octane numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asinger,_Friedrich_1967_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Asinger,_Friedrich_1967-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_reactions">Other reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Other reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Alkanes will react with <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">steam</a> in the presence of a <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a> to give <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>. Alkanes can be <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Chlorosulfonation&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Chlorosulfonation (page does not exist)">chlorosulfonated</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitration" title="Nitration">nitrated</a>, although both reactions require special conditions. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a> of alkanes to <a href="/wiki/Carboxylic_acid" title="Carboxylic acid">carboxylic acids</a> is of some technical importance. In the <a href="/wiki/Reed_reaction" title="Reed reaction">Reed reaction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photochemistry" title="Photochemistry">light</a> convert hydrocarbons to <a href="/wiki/Sulfonic_acid" title="Sulfonic acid">sulfonyl chlorides</a>. <a href="/wiki/Nucleophilic_abstraction" title="Nucleophilic abstraction">Nucleophilic Abstraction</a> can be used to separate an alkane from a metal. Alkyl groups can be transferred from one compound to another by <a href="/wiki/Transmetalation" title="Transmetalation">transmetalation</a> reactions.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence_of_alkanes_in_the_Universe">Occurrence of alkanes in the Universe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Occurrence of alkanes in the Universe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="840" data-file-width="840" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Jupiter.jpg/220px-Jupiter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Jupiter.jpg/330px-Jupiter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Jupiter.jpg/440px-Jupiter.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">Methane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a> make up a tiny proportion of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>'s atmosphere</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oil_well.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Oil_well.jpg/220px-Oil_well.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Oil_well.jpg/330px-Oil_well.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Oil_well.jpg/440px-Oil_well.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oil_well.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Extraction of oil, which contains many different <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> including alkanes</div></div></div>
<p>Alkanes form a small portion of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_body_atmosphere" title="Celestial body atmosphere">atmospheres</a> of the outer gas planets such as <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> (0.1% methane, 2 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a> ethane), <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> (0.2% methane, 5 ppm ethane), <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> (1.99% methane, 2.5 ppm ethane) and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> (1.5% methane, 1.5 ppm ethane). <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a> (1.6% methane), a satellite of Saturn, was examined by the <a href="/wiki/Huygens_(spacecraft)" title="Huygens (spacecraft)"><i>Huygens</i> probe</a>, which indicated that Titan's atmosphere periodically rains liquid methane onto the moon's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Also on Titan the Cassini mission has imaged seasonal methane/ethane lakes near the polar regions of Titan. <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">Methane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a> have also been detected in the tail of the comet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyakutake" title="Hyakutake">Hyakutake</a>. Chemical analysis showed that the abundances of ethane and methane were roughly equal, which is thought to imply that its ices formed in interstellar space, away from the Sun, which would have evaporated these volatile molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-science-19">[19]</a></sup> Alkanes have also been detected in <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Carbonaceous_chondrite" title="Carbonaceous chondrite">carbonaceous chondrites</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence_of_alkanes_on_Earth">Occurrence of alkanes on Earth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Occurrence of alkanes on Earth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Traces of methane gas (about 0.0002% or 1745 ppb) occur in the Earth's atmosphere, produced primarily by <a href="/wiki/Methanogenesis" title="Methanogenesis">methanogenic</a> microorganisms, such as <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">Archaea</a> in the gut of ruminants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janssen_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Janssen-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most important commercial sources for alkanes are natural gas and <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">oil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m&amp;b_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-m&amp;b-14">[14]</a></sup> Natural gas contains primarily methane and ethane, with some <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Butane" title="Butane">butane</a>: oil is a mixture of liquid alkanes and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrocarbons" title="Hydrocarbons">hydrocarbons</a>. These hydrocarbons were formed when marine animals and plants (zooplankton and phytoplankton) died and sank to the bottom of ancient seas and were covered with sediments in an <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/anoxic" title="wikt:anoxic">anoxic</a> environment and converted over many millions of years at high temperatures and high pressure to their current form. Natural gas resulted thereby for example from the following reaction:
</p>
<dl><dd>C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>12</sub>O<sub>6</sub> → 3 CH<sub>4</sub> + 3 CO<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>These hydrocarbon deposits, collected in porous rocks trapped beneath impermeable cap rocks, comprise commercial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_fields" title="Oil fields">oil fields</a>. They have formed over millions of years and once exhausted cannot be readily replaced. The depletion of these hydrocarbons reserves is the basis for what is known as the <a href="/wiki/Energy_crisis" title="Energy crisis">energy crisis</a>.
</p><p>Methane is also present in what is called <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a>, produced by animals and decaying matter, which is a possible <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_source" title="Renewable energy source">renewable energy source</a>.
</p><p>Alkanes have a low solubility in water, so the content in the oceans is negligible; however, at high pressures and low temperatures (such as at the bottom of the oceans), methane can co-crystallize with water to form a solid <a href="/wiki/Methane_clathrate" title="Methane clathrate">methane clathrate</a> (methane hydrate). Although this cannot be commercially exploited at the present time, the amount of combustible energy of the known methane clathrate fields exceeds the energy content of all the natural gas and oil deposits put together. Methane extracted from methane clathrate is, therefore, a candidate for future fuels.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_occurrence">Biological occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Biological occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Acyclic alkanes occur in nature in various ways.
</p>
<dl><dt>Bacteria and archaea</dt></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rotbuntes_Rind.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1280" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Rotbuntes_Rind.jpg/220px-Rotbuntes_Rind.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Rotbuntes_Rind.jpg/330px-Rotbuntes_Rind.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Rotbuntes_Rind.jpg/440px-Rotbuntes_Rind.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rotbuntes_Rind.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">Methanogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a> in the gut of this cow are responsible for some of the <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> in Earth's atmosphere.</div></div></div>
<p>Certain types of bacteria can metabolize alkanes: they prefer even-numbered carbon chains as they are easier to degrade than odd-numbered chains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the other hand, certain <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogens</a>, produce large quantities of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> by the metabolism of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> or other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidized</a> organic compounds. The energy is released by the oxidation of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>CO<sub>2</sub> + 4 H<sub>2</sub> → CH<sub>4</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>Methanogens are also the producers of <a href="/wiki/Marsh_gas" title="Marsh gas">marsh gas</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wetlands" title="Wetlands">wetlands</a>, and release about two billion tonnes of methane per year<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>—the atmospheric content of this gas is produced nearly exclusively by them. The methane output of <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivores</a>, which can release 30 to 50 gallons per day,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> and of <a href="/wiki/Termite" title="Termite">termites</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> is also due to methanogens. They also produce this simplest of all alkanes in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intestine" title="Intestine">intestines</a> of humans. Methanogenic archaea are, hence, at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>, with carbon being released back into the atmosphere after having been fixed by <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>. It is probable that our current deposits of natural gas were formed in a similar way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Fungi and plants</dt></dl>
<p>Alkanes also play a role, if a minor role, in the biology of the three <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotic</a> groups of organisms: <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a>, plants and animals. Some specialized yeasts, e.g., <i>Candida tropicale</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Pichia" title="Pichia">Pichia</a></i> sp., <i><a href="/wiki/Rhodotorula" title="Rhodotorula">Rhodotorula</a></i> sp., can use alkanes as a source of carbon or energy. The fungus <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amorphotheca_resinae" title="Amorphotheca resinae">Amorphotheca resinae</a></i> prefers the longer-chain alkanes in <a href="/wiki/Aviation_fuel" title="Aviation fuel">aviation fuel</a>, and can cause serious problems for aircraft in tropical regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hendey_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hendey-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>In plants, the solid long-chain alkanes are found in the <a href="/wiki/Plant_cuticle" title="Plant cuticle">plant cuticle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epicuticular_wax" title="Epicuticular wax">epicuticular wax</a> of many species, but are only rarely major constituents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baker1982_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baker1982-27">[27]</a></sup> They protect the plant against water loss, prevent the <a href="/wiki/Leaching_(agriculture)" title="Leaching (agriculture)">leaching</a> of important minerals by the rain, and protect against bacteria, fungi, and harmful insects. The carbon chains in plant alkanes are usually odd-numbered, between 27 and 33 carbon atoms in length<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baker1982_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Baker1982-27">[27]</a></sup> and are made by the plants by <a href="/wiki/Decarboxylation" title="Decarboxylation">decarboxylation</a> of even-numbered <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a>. The exact composition of the layer of wax is not only species-dependent but changes also with the season and such environmental factors as lighting conditions, temperature or humidity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baker1982_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-Baker1982-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>More volatile short-chain alkanes are also produced by and found in plant tissues. The <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_pine" title="Jeffrey pine">Jeffrey pine</a> is noted for producing exceptionally high levels of <i>n</i>-<a href="/wiki/Heptane" title="Heptane">heptane</a> in its resin, for which reason its distillate was designated as the zero point for one <a href="/wiki/Octane_rating" title="Octane rating">octane rating</a>. Floral scents have also long been known to contain volatile alkane components, and <i>n</i>-<a href="/wiki/Nonane" title="Nonane">nonane</a> is a significant component in the scent of some <a href="/wiki/Rose" title="Rose">roses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Emission of gaseous and volatile alkanes such as <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pentane" title="Pentane">pentane</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hexane" title="Hexane">hexane</a> by plants has also been documented at low levels, though they are not generally considered to be a major component of biogenic air pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Edible vegetable oils also typically contain small fractions of biogenic alkanes with a wide spectrum of carbon numbers, mainly 8 to 35, usually peaking in the low to upper 20s, with concentrations up to dozens of milligrams per kilogram (parts per million by weight) and sometimes over a hundred for the total alkane fraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Animals</dt></dl>
<p>Alkanes are found in animal products, although they are less important than unsaturated hydrocarbons. One example is the shark liver oil, which is approximately 14% <a href="/wiki/Pristane" title="Pristane">pristane</a> (2,6,10,14-tetramethylpentadecane, C<sub>19</sub>H<sub>40</sub>). They are important as <a href="/wiki/Pheromone" title="Pheromone">pheromones</a>, chemical messenger materials, on which insects depend for communication. In some species, e.g. the support beetle <i><a href="/wiki/Xylotrechus_colonus" title="Xylotrechus colonus">Xylotrechus colonus</a></i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pentacosane" title="Pentacosane">pentacosane</a> (C<sub>25</sub>H<sub>52</sub>), 3-methylpentaicosane (C<sub>26</sub>H<sub>54</sub>) and 9-methylpentaicosane (C<sub>26</sub>H<sub>54</sub>) are transferred by body contact. With others like the <a href="/wiki/Tsetse_fly" title="Tsetse fly">tsetse fly</a> <i>Glossina morsitans morsitans</i>, the pheromone contains the four alkanes 2-methylheptadecane (C<sub>18</sub>H<sub>38</sub>), 17,21-dimethylheptatriacontane (C<sub>39</sub>H<sub>80</sub>), 15,19-dimethylheptatriacontane (C<sub>39</sub>H<sub>80</sub>) and 15,19,23-trimethylheptatriacontane (C<sub>40</sub>H<sub>82</sub>), and acts by smell over longer distances. <a href="/wiki/Waggle_dance" title="Waggle dance">Waggle-dancing</a> <a href="/wiki/Honey_bee" title="Honey bee">honey bees</a> produce and release two alkanes, tricosane and pentacosane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecological_relations">Ecological relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Ecological relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ophrys_sphegodes_flower.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="795" data-file-width="685" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Ophrys_sphegodes_flower.jpg/220px-Ophrys_sphegodes_flower.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Ophrys_sphegodes_flower.jpg/330px-Ophrys_sphegodes_flower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Ophrys_sphegodes_flower.jpg/440px-Ophrys_sphegodes_flower.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ophrys_sphegodes_flower.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early spider orchid (<i><a href="/wiki/Ophrys_sphegodes" title="Ophrys sphegodes">Ophrys sphegodes</a></i>)</div></div></div>
<p>One example, in which both plant and animal alkanes play a role, is the ecological relationship between the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sand_bee&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sand bee (page does not exist)">sand bee</a> (<i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Andrena_nigroaenea&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Andrena nigroaenea (page does not exist)">Andrena nigroaenea</a></i>) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_spider_orchid" title="Early spider orchid">early spider orchid</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Ophrys_sphegodes" title="Ophrys sphegodes">Ophrys sphegodes</a></i>); the latter is dependent for <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">pollination</a> on the former. Sand bees use pheromones in order to identify a mate; in the case of <i>A. nigroaenea</i>, the females emit a mixture of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tricosane" title="Tricosane">tricosane</a> (C<sub>23</sub>H<sub>48</sub>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pentacosane" title="Pentacosane">pentacosane</a> (C<sub>25</sub>H<sub>52</sub>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heptacosane" title="Heptacosane">heptacosane</a> (C<sub>27</sub>H<sub>56</sub>) in the ratio 3:3:1, and males are attracted by specifically this odor. The orchid takes advantage of this mating arrangement to get the male bee to collect and disseminate its pollen; parts of its flower not only resemble the appearance of sand bees but also produce large quantities of the three alkanes in the same ratio as female sand bees. As a result, numerous males are lured to the blooms and attempt to copulate with their imaginary partner: although this endeavor is not crowned with success for the bee, it allows the orchid to transfer its pollen,
which will be dispersed after the departure of the frustrated male to different blooms.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Petroleum_refining">Petroleum refining</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Petroleum refining">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ShellMartinez-refi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="800" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/ShellMartinez-refi.jpg/220px-ShellMartinez-refi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/ShellMartinez-refi.jpg/330px-ShellMartinez-refi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/ShellMartinez-refi.jpg/440px-ShellMartinez-refi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ShellMartinez-refi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Oil_refinery" title="Oil refinery">oil refinery</a> at <a href="/wiki/Martinez,_California" title="Martinez, California">Martinez, California</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>As stated earlier, the most important source of alkanes is natural gas and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crude_oil" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m&amp;b_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-m&amp;b-14">[14]</a></sup> Alkanes are separated in an <a href="/wiki/Oil_refinery" title="Oil refinery">oil refinery</a> by <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">fractional distillation</a> and processed into many different products.
</p>
<h3><span id="Fischer.E2.80.93Tropsch"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Fischer–Tropsch">Fischer–Tropsch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Fischer–Tropsch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Fischer%E2%80%93Tropsch_process" title="Fischer–Tropsch process">Fischer–Tropsch process</a> is a method to synthesize liquid hydrocarbons, including alkanes, from <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> and hydrogen. This method is used to produce substitutes for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petroleum_distillate" title="Petroleum distillate">petroleum distillates</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laboratory_preparation">Laboratory preparation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Laboratory preparation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is usually little need for alkanes to be synthesized in the laboratory, since they are usually commercially available. Also, alkanes are generally unreactive chemically or biologically, and do not undergo <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Functional_group_interconversion" title="Functional group interconversion">functional group interconversions</a> cleanly. When alkanes are produced in the laboratory, it is often a side-product of a reaction. For example, the use of <a href="/wiki/N-Butyllithium" title="N-Butyllithium"><i>n</i>-butyllithium</a> as a strong <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">base</a> gives the conjugate acid, <i>n</i>-butane as a side-product:
</p>
<dl><dd>C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>9</sub>Li + H<sub>2</sub>O → C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>10</sub> + <a href="/wiki/Lithium_hydroxide" title="Lithium hydroxide">LiOH</a></dd></dl>
<p>However, at times it may be desirable to make a section of a molecule into an alkane-like functionality (<a href="/wiki/Alkyl" title="Alkyl">alkyl</a> group) using the above or similar methods. For example, an <a href="/wiki/Ethyl_group" title="Ethyl group">ethyl group</a> is an alkyl group; when this is attached to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxy</a> group, it gives <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, which is not an alkane. To do so, the best-known methods are <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Alkene" title="Alkene">alkenes</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>RCH=CH<sub>2</sub> + H<sub>2</sub> → RCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>3</sub><span class="nowrap">     </span>(R = <a href="/wiki/Alkyl" title="Alkyl">alkyl</a>)</dd></dl>
<p>Alkanes or alkyl groups can also be prepared directly from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkyl_halide" title="Alkyl halide">alkyl halides</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Corey%E2%80%93House_synthesis" title="Corey–House synthesis">Corey–House–Posner–Whitesides reaction</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Barton%E2%80%93McCombie_deoxygenation" title="Barton–McCombie deoxygenation">Barton–McCombie deoxygenation</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> removes hydroxyl groups from alcohols e.g.
</p>
<dl><dd><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Barton-McCombie_Deoxygenation_Scheme.svg" title="Barton–McCombie deoxygenation scheme"><img alt="Barton–McCombie deoxygenation scheme" data-file-height="159" data-file-width="886" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Barton-McCombie_Deoxygenation_Scheme.svg/600px-Barton-McCombie_Deoxygenation_Scheme.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Barton-McCombie_Deoxygenation_Scheme.svg/900px-Barton-McCombie_Deoxygenation_Scheme.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Barton-McCombie_Deoxygenation_Scheme.svg/1200px-Barton-McCombie_Deoxygenation_Scheme.svg.png 2x" width="600"/></a></dd></dl>
<p>and the <a href="/wiki/Clemmensen_reduction" title="Clemmensen reduction">Clemmensen reduction</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> removes carbonyl groups from aldehydes and ketones to form alkanes or alkyl-substituted compounds e.g.:
</p>
<dl><dd><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clemmensen_Reduction_Scheme.png" title="Clemmensen Reduction"><img alt="Clemmensen Reduction" data-file-height="196" data-file-width="773" height="63" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Clemmensen_Reduction_Scheme.png/250px-Clemmensen_Reduction_Scheme.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Clemmensen_Reduction_Scheme.png/375px-Clemmensen_Reduction_Scheme.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Clemmensen_Reduction_Scheme.png/500px-Clemmensen_Reduction_Scheme.png 2x" width="250"/></a></dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The applications of alkanes depend on the number of carbon atoms. The first four alkanes are used mainly for heating and cooking purposes, and in some countries for electricity generation. <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">Methane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a> are the main components of natural gas; they are normally stored as gases under pressure. It is, however, easier to transport them as liquids: This requires both compression and cooling of the gas.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">Propane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Butane" title="Butane">butane</a> are gases at atmospheric pressure that can be liquefied at fairly low pressures and are commonly known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquified_petroleum_gas" title="Liquified petroleum gas">liquified petroleum gas</a> (LPG). Propane is used in propane gas burners and as a fuel for road vehicles,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> butane in space heaters and disposable cigarette lighters. Both are used as propellants in <a href="/wiki/Aerosol_spray" title="Aerosol spray">aerosol sprays</a>.
</p><p>From <a href="/wiki/Pentane" title="Pentane">pentane</a> to <a href="/wiki/Octane" title="Octane">octane</a> the alkanes are highly volatile liquids. They are used as fuels in <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engines</a>, as they vaporize easily on entry into the combustion chamber without forming droplets, which would impair the uniformity of the combustion. Branched-chain alkanes are preferred as they are much less prone to premature ignition, which causes <a href="/wiki/Engine_knocking" title="Engine knocking">knocking</a>, than their straight-chain homologues. This propensity to premature ignition is measured by the <a href="/wiki/Octane_rating" title="Octane rating">octane rating</a> of the fuel, where <a href="/wiki/2,2,4-Trimethylpentane" title="2,2,4-Trimethylpentane">2,2,4-trimethylpentane</a> (<i>isooctane</i>) has an arbitrary value of 100, and <a href="/wiki/Heptane" title="Heptane">heptane</a> has a value of zero. Apart from their use as fuels, the middle alkanes are also good <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvents</a> for nonpolar substances.
</p><p>Alkanes from <a href="/wiki/Nonane" title="Nonane">nonane</a> to, for instance, <a href="/wiki/Hexadecane" title="Hexadecane">hexadecane</a> (an alkane with sixteen carbon atoms) are liquids of higher <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a>, less and less suitable for use in gasoline. They form instead the major part of <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aviation_fuel" title="Aviation fuel">aviation fuel</a>. Diesel fuels are characterized by their <a href="/wiki/Cetane_number" title="Cetane number">cetane number</a>, cetane being an old name for hexadecane. However, the higher melting points of these alkanes can cause problems at low temperatures and in polar regions, where the fuel becomes too thick to flow correctly.
</p><p>Alkanes from hexadecane upwards form the most important components of <a href="/wiki/Fuel_oil" title="Fuel oil">fuel oil</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lubricating_oil" title="Lubricating oil">lubricating oil</a>. In the latter function, they work at the same time as anti-corrosive agents, as their hydrophobic nature means that water cannot reach the metal surface. Many solid alkanes find use as <a href="/wiki/Paraffin_wax" title="Paraffin wax">paraffin wax</a>, for example, in <a href="/wiki/Candle" title="Candle">candles</a>. This should not be confused however with true <a href="/wiki/Wax" title="Wax">wax</a>, which consists primarily of <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esters</a>.
</p><p>Alkanes with a chain length of approximately 35 or more carbon atoms are found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitumen" title="Bitumen">bitumen</a>, used, for example, in road surfacing. However, the higher alkanes have little value and are usually split into lower alkanes by <a href="/wiki/Cracking_(chemistry)" title="Cracking (chemistry)">cracking</a>.
</p><p>Some synthetic <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Polyethylene" title="Polyethylene">polyethylene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polypropylene" title="Polypropylene">polypropylene</a> are alkanes with chains containing hundreds of thousands of carbon atoms. These materials are used in innumerable applications, and billions of kilograms of these materials are made and used each year.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_transformations">Environmental transformations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Environmental transformations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(August 2014)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Alkanes are chemically very inert apolar molecules which are not very reactive as organic compounds.  This inertness yields serious ecological issues if they are released into the environment. Due to their lack of functional groups and low water solubility, alkanes show poor bioavailability for microorganisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are, however, some microorganisms possessing the metabolic capacity to utilize n-alkanes as both carbon and energy sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Some bacterial species are highly specialised in degrading alkanes; these are referred to as hydrocarbonoclastic bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hazards">Hazards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Hazards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alkane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(September 2017)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Methane is flammable, explosive and dangerous to inhale, because it is a colorless, odorless gas, special caution must be taken around methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-42">[42]</a></sup> Ethane is also extremely flammable, dangerous to inhale and explosive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-43">[43]</a></sup> Both of these may cause suffocation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-43">[43]</a></sup> Similarly, propane is flammable and explosive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-44">[44]</a></sup> It may cause drowsiness or unconsciousness if inhaled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-44">[44]</a></sup> Butane has the same hazards to consider as propane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alkanes also pose a threat to the environment. Branched alkanes have a lower biodegradability than unbranched alkanes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> However, methane is ranked as the most dangerous greenhouse gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-47">[47]</a></sup> Although the amount of methane in the atmosphere is low, it does pose a threat to the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>

<li><a href="/wiki/Nonadecane" title="Nonadecane">Nonadecane</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">19</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sub></span></span>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Icosane" title="Icosane">Icosane</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">20</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">42</sub></span></span>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Heneicosane" title="Heneicosane">Heneicosane</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">21</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">44</sub></span></span>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tetracosane" title="Tetracosane">Tetracosane</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">24</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">50</sub></span></span>)</li>
